  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ffbbd66e-afd2-4970-a1f1-54d39b347365'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3928688073651890495"><i>Manfred</i> contains supernatural elements, in keeping with the popularity of the ghost story in England at the time. It is a typical example of a Romantic closet drama. Manfred was adapted musically by Robert Schumann in 1852, in a composition entitled Manfred: Dramatic Poem with music in Three Parts, and later by Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky in his Manfred Symphony, Op. 58, as w</span>
  <span id="freeText3928688073651890495" style="display:none"><i>Manfred</i> contains supernatural elements, in keeping with the popularity of the ghost story in England at the time. It is a typical example of a Romantic closet drama. Manfred was adapted musically by Robert Schumann in 1852, in a composition entitled Manfred: Dramatic Poem with music in Three Parts, and later by Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky in his Manfred Symphony, Op. 58, as well as by Carl Reinecke. Friedrich Nietzsche was impressed by the poem's depiction of a super-human being, and wrote some music for it. <br /><br />Byron wrote this "metaphysical drama", as he called it, after his marriage failed in scandal amidst charges of sexual improprieties and an incestuous affair between Byron and his half-sister, Augusta Leigh. Attacked by the press and ostracized by London society, Byron fled England for Switzerland in 1816 and never returned. Because Manfred was written immediately after this and because Manfred regards a main character tortured by his own sense of guilt for an unmentionable offense, some critics consider Manfred to be autobiographical, or even confessional.<br /><br />The unnamed but forbidden nature of Manfred's relationship to Astarte is believed to represent Byron's relationship with his half-sister Augusta. Byron commenced this work in late 1816, only a few months after the famed ghost-story sessions which provided the initial impetus for Mary Shelley's Frankenstein. The supernatural references are made clear throughout the poem. In one scene, for example, (Act III, Scene IV, Interior of the Tower), Manfred recalls traveling through time (or astral projection traveling) to Caesar's palace, "and fill'd up, As 't were anew, the gaps of centuries...".</span>
  <a data-text-id="3928688073651890495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

      <h2 class='buyButtonContainer__title u-inlineBlock'%>Get A Copy</h2>
    </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer199190124271763354">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1981/02/corny-not-sublime-manfred-by-lord-byron.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/><br/>Corny, not the Sublime: "Manfred" by Lord Byron<br/><br/><br/>(Original Review, 1981-02-10)<br/><br/><br/><br/>It has been a long time since I read “Manfred”, and much longer since “Paradise Lost”, so maybe I am wrong. But Milton's Satan was first and foremost, I think, rebellious. Satan's will was his own, NOT God's, he was so to speak his own man. He could not regain Paradise because wherever he went, Hell went. Satan in Paradise is Satan still in Hell, "myself am Hel</span>
  <span id="freeText199190124271763354" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1981/02/corny-not-sublime-manfred-by-lord-byron.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Corny, not the Sublime: "Manfred" by Lord Byron<br /><br /><br />(Original Review, 1981-02-10)<br /><br /><br /><br />It has been a long time since I read “Manfred”, and much longer since “Paradise Lost”, so maybe I am wrong. But Milton's Satan was first and foremost, I think, rebellious. Satan's will was his own, NOT God's, he was so to speak his own man. He could not regain Paradise because wherever he went, Hell went. Satan in Paradise is Satan still in Hell, "myself am Hell".</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2646750967611651016">This was not a bad dramatic poem or play, but after reading Byron's wonderful Cain, this didn't quite compare to me. <br/><br/>I wish I just knew what he did to his wife that was so terrible and made him harbour such guilt.   <span id="freeText2646750967611651016" style="display:none">This was not a bad dramatic poem or play, but after reading Byron's wonderful Cain, this didn't quite compare to me. <br /><br />I wish I just knew what he did to his wife that was so terrible and made him harbour such guilt. I know some of the magic should have been in the mystery but I wanted to know... which aligned me with Manfred's previous ambition and knowledge hungry self, I suppose. Smart.<br /><br />My version of this had two final acts. It had Act three unedited, and the edited and revised version of act three. I much preferred the revised and edited ending as it gave Manfred more agency. <br /><br />Yeah, I really don't have much more to say. A good enough play, just not great. <br /><br />I read this for class.</span>

Often described as the most flamboyant and notorious of the major Romantics, Byron remains widely read and influential. Byron wrote this "metaphysical drama", as he called it, after his marriage failed in scandal amidst charges of sexual improprieties and an incestuous affair between Byron and his half-sister, Augusta Leigh. Attacked by the press and ostracised by London society, Byron fled England for Switzerland in 1816 and never returned. Manfred was written immediately after this.</i></span>

<div id="review_2096694900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2096694900" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5863991-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451268976p2/5863991.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2096694900?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/5863991-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781609775742" href="/book/show/21891060-manfred">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=back-2-the-classics-2018">back-2-the-classics-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=aab-read-the-name-2018">aab-read-the-name-2018</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=classics-club">classics-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5863991-beth?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2096694900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7911100354706983959">I'm not sure I had heard of this play until I came across a reference to it in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201.Jonathan_Strange___Mr_Norrell' title='Jonathan Strange & Mr Norrell by Susanna Clarke' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jonathan Strange & Mr Norrell</a>. It is not mentioned by name, but here's the relevant bit: <br/><br/>" 'Now I know very little of English magicians. They have always seemed to me a parcel of dull, dusty old men — except for John Uskglass. He is quite another matter! The magician who tamed the Otherlanders! The only magician to defeat death! The magician whom Lucifer himself was forced to treat as an equal! Now whenever Strange co</span>
  <span id="freeText7911100354706983959" style="display:none">I'm not sure I had heard of this play until I came across a reference to it in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201.Jonathan_Strange___Mr_Norrell" title="Jonathan Strange &amp; Mr Norrell by Susanna Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Strange &amp; Mr Norrell</a>. It is not mentioned by name, but here's the relevant bit: <br /><br />" 'Now I know very little of English magicians. They have always seemed to me a parcel of dull, dusty old men — except for John Uskglass. He is quite another matter! The magician who tamed the Otherlanders! The only magician to defeat death! The magician whom Lucifer himself was forced to treat as an equal! Now whenever Strange compares himself to this sublime being — as he must from time to time — he sees himself for what he truly is: a plodding earthbound mediocrity! All his achievements — so praised in the desolate little isle — crumble to dust before him! That will bring on as fine a bout of despair as you could wish to see. <i>This is to be mortal, and seek the things beyond mortality.'</i> <br /><br />Lord Byron paused for a moment, as if committing the last remark to memory in case he should want to put it in a poem… <br /><br />[later:]<br /><br />'I am by chance writing a poem about a magician who wrestles with the Ineffable Spirits who rule his destiny. Of course, as a model for my magician Strange is far from perfect — he lacks the true heroic nature; for that I shall be obliged to put in something of myself.' "<br /><br />***<br /><br />Manfred is a verse drama published in 1817. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord Byron</a> started writing it a few months after the famous ghost-story sessions with Percy Shelley and Mary Shelley that provided the inspiration for Frankenstein. I've never read much of Byron before this, except for a few of his poems in anthologies. This is longer, but still fairly short, so it's a good one to read if you want to get a taste of Byron without reading the much longer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78249.Don_Juan" title="Don Juan by Lord Byron" rel="nofollow noopener">Don Juan</a>.<br /><br />Count Manfred is a Swiss nobleman tortured by mysterious guilt for a sin that is somehow connected to the death of his beloved, Astarte. He summons seven spirits to grant him forgetfulness: the spirits of air, mountains, ocean, earth, winds, night, and of Manfred’s own guiding star. Most of the play is in blank verse, although the spirits have rhyming dialogue. I was completely captivated! I loved this mix of the Gothic and the Romantic. It probably draws some inspiration from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a> and/or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11155.Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe" rel="nofollow noopener">Christopher Marlowe</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18525.Dr__Faustus" title="Dr. Faustus by Christopher Marlowe" rel="nofollow noopener">Dr. Faustus</a>, but it also feels like its own thing. (I read part 1 of Faust years ago; I just don't have it listed on GoodReads, since I haven't added most of the books I read before joining the site.)<br /><br />I listened to the LibriVox audio recording after reading this. It's an excellent reading. Highly recommended! <br /><br />The mind which is immortal makes itself<br />Requital for its good or evil thoughts, <br />Is its own origin of ill and end,<br />And its own place and time; its innate sense,<br />When stripped of this mortality, derives<br />No colour from the fleeting things without,<br />But is absorb'd in sufferance or in joy,<br />Born from the knowledge of its own desert.” <br />―<br /><br />(I think this quote is saying something like: self-knowledge is its own punishment -- or its own reward, depending on whether it leads to "sufferance" or "joy.")<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[What happened? possibly he had an incestous relationship with her and she committed suicide? I’m thinking of this bit: <br /><i><br />my blood! the pure warm stream<br />Which ran in the veins of my fathers, and in ours<br />When we were in our youth, and had one heart<br />And loved each other as we should not love</i><br /><br />"our" refers to both of them, so the wording here seems to imply that his blood is also hers, i.e., they’re related. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="7911100354706983959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_277588777" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/277588777" />
    <a title="Miguel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6164326-miguel"><img alt="Miguel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520452081p2/6164326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/277588777?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miguel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miguel" href="/user/show/6164326-miguel">Miguel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277588777" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8033233014964205858">The Byronic hero is deftly explored here, as in Childe Harold, but in a way which lends to a reading that this poem is Byron's confession of his forbidden love affair with his half-sister Augusta. But what is there to say about this classic, really? The prose is arresting and Manfred is inspiring, a character through life found no affinity with human beings and bucked the status-quo at every turn. Manfred strives to be the quintessential Nietzschean superman, valuing the forgetfulness of past in</span>
  <span id="freeText8033233014964205858" style="display:none">The Byronic hero is deftly explored here, as in Childe Harold, but in a way which lends to a reading that this poem is Byron's confession of his forbidden love affair with his half-sister Augusta. But what is there to say about this classic, really? The prose is arresting and Manfred is inspiring, a character through life found no affinity with human beings and bucked the status-quo at every turn. Manfred strives to be the quintessential Nietzschean superman, valuing the forgetfulness of past indiscretions, but falling short of that ideal and in turn seeking his own death through his own agency rather than at the hands of any spirit. Manfred lives alone and on his own terms and seeks to die the same way. Ultimately Manfred is an astonishing tale about self-sufficiency and isolation and where the two meet, with all the trappings of Romantic fiction, extolling nature and the intuitive rather than the precisely logical.<br /><br />It's a short read with a great deal to offer for anyone who has ever felt alone in a society that means nothing to them. And, of course, any students of Romantic fiction would certainly be assigned this piece. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8033233014964205858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2761467632" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14925529424682847497">This is the locus classicus of the brooding - male - outsider, haunted by death and beauty while precariously perched on a high rock above swirling mists and surrounded by indifferent, craggy alpine peaks. Byron's Manfred was published in 1817. Caspar David Friedrich painted his 'Walker above the mists' in 1818. The former offers the more incisive portrait, I feel, particularly in the second scene when Manfred, sick at heart and despondent, muses on the human predicament of having to contend wit</span>
  <span id="freeText14925529424682847497" style="display:none">This is the locus classicus of the brooding - male - outsider, haunted by death and beauty while precariously perched on a high rock above swirling mists and surrounded by indifferent, craggy alpine peaks. Byron's Manfred was published in 1817. Caspar David Friedrich painted his 'Walker above the mists' in 1818. The former offers the more incisive portrait, I feel, particularly in the second scene when Manfred, sick at heart and despondent, muses on the human predicament of having to contend with 'low wants and lofty will'. Inevitably I am thinking of Tchaikovsky here, another 'poète maudit' who felt trapped between these conflicting impulses, relied on Manfred as the seed for a major symphonic work, and eventually killed himself. The composer will have shuddered at Manfred's vision that connects human fate to the ineffability of sound. <br /><br /><i>Hark! The note,</i><br />[The Shepherd's pipe in the distance is heard.]<br /><i>The natural music of the mountain reed -<br />For here the patriarchal days are not<br />A pastoral fable - pipes in the liberal air, <br />Mixed with the sweet bells of the sauntering herd; <br />My soul would drink those echoes. Oh, that I were<br />The viewless spirit of a lovely sound, <br />A living voice, a breathing harmony, <br />A bodiless enjoyment - born and dying<br />With the blest tone which made me!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14925529424682847497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2779953355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2779953355" />
    <a title="Freddie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37862146-freddie"><img alt="Freddie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2779953355?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Freddie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Freddie" href="/user/show/37862146-freddie">Freddie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2779953355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16646976809085440492">I can understand why students are so reluctant to engage classical literature now. It's not that they don't respect the canon or hate reading, but rather it's the opposite. Manfred uses archaic language (even for its time), a high register, roundabout and very literary language, historical allusions, and more that would make the poem hard to digest at a glance. Of course even an untrained reader could extract the rhythm of the poem and glean that Byron is quite a skilled writer. Luckily I read t</span>
  <span id="freeText16646976809085440492" style="display:none">I can understand why students are so reluctant to engage classical literature now. It's not that they don't respect the canon or hate reading, but rather it's the opposite. Manfred uses archaic language (even for its time), a high register, roundabout and very literary language, historical allusions, and more that would make the poem hard to digest at a glance. Of course even an untrained reader could extract the rhythm of the poem and glean that Byron is quite a skilled writer. Luckily I read the poem within the Norton Critical Edition of Byron's works and there were helpful footnotes, as well as context.<br /><br />Byron's Manfred is quite clearly a tortured soul. He has claimed responsibility for somehow or another killing the one love of his life. This leads him to dabble in unholy arts, spending his time amongst bones and in communion with spirits. We're introduced to him at the height of this despair and his power over nature. He binds spirits. He cannot die. All who witness him can't help but noting his noble qualities. He naturally evokes our pity, but since most of us can't fathom the extent of his sorrow, his angst can come off as overdramatic.<br /><br />The poem is actually quite short, but the stanzas can be long. The little action that happens is that Manfred is unable to bring his love back to life, and when calling upon her, she tells him that his troubles will end. At the end, he reproachfully accepts his death, shunning both the spiritual guide as well as the priest that offer him their help. He chooses to quit his life on his own terms, finally attaining mortality.<br /><br />There's certainly a lot of narrative depth that will require multiple readings. Manfred is a complex character who, while quite explicit about his feelings, is played upon by various forces. There's also the question of what really happened to his love and what elicits the reaction of her spirit. This is the first Byron I read (aside from bits of The Vision of Judgment) and I can't wait to read Don Juan.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16646976809085440492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2870069311" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2870069311" />
    <a title="Rita Neves" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54199354-rita-neves"><img alt="Rita Neves" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503345419p2/54199354.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_2681766211" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2681766211" />
    <a title="Jacques Coulardeau" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24373332-jacques-coulardeau"><img alt="Jacques Coulardeau" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476373508p2/24373332.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2681766211?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacques Coulardeau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacques Coulardeau" href="/user/show/24373332-jacques-coulardeau">Jacques Coulardeau</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2681766211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14850176354849181903">PREACHING SUICIDE FROM THE TOP OF THE MOUNTAIN<br/><br/>The poem is strange in the fact it is a dramatic poem, with many voices after all, hence very theatrical. The various voices are made clear in the versification, from free verse for Manfred himself and some otters to versified contributions of others like spirits and supernatural beings. Most of these spirits are identified in what they say apart from some who have some kind of names. The first seven spirits called by Manfred are the spirits of “eart</span>
  <span id="freeText14850176354849181903" style="display:none">PREACHING SUICIDE FROM THE TOP OF THE MOUNTAIN<br /><br />The poem is strange in the fact it is a dramatic poem, with many voices after all, hence very theatrical. The various voices are made clear in the versification, from free verse for Manfred himself and some otters to versified contributions of others like spirits and supernatural beings. Most of these spirits are identified in what they say apart from some who have some kind of names. The first seven spirits called by Manfred are the spirits of “earth, ocean, air, night, mountains, winds and [Manfred’s] star.” We have, in this opening, the supernatural spirits of things seen in a very romantic approach, everything in the world is spiritual. Every natural element is animated by a specific spirit. We are in Faust, we are in Werther. Perfect continuation. But seven is, of course, a Christian concept, the Holy Week, the genesis of the universe, but it also has a wider spiritual value in many cultures. It is yet very romantic because one spirit for the “winds” is in complete contradiction with all mythologies for which there are always four winds identified by the four cardinal points. For another tradition, that of Buddhism, air and wind are only one element because air is movement because of the wind. And the wind is then in the singular. The spirit of mountains is definitely in phase with other oriental or Amerindian mythologies, though such animation of mountains is probably in tradition with old pre-Ice-Age beliefs and mythologies in Europe.<br /><br />Among these spirits, the three Destinies are one identity of the triple goddess that has many forms from the Erinyes to the Harpies and of course the famous triple goddess in Greek tradition and in Germanic tradition. But when all the spirits, plus Nemesis and Arimanes come together we reach a total of twelve supernatural beings, which is also very common in many mythologies and crucial in Christianity. But Manfred breaks this symbolism – the way he refuses the intercession of the Bishop – by invoking and bringing into this gathering a thirteenth spirit, that of Astarte. The name is surprising since she is an impersonation of some warfare goddess in the Middle East, and among Celts. But we understand that she is the “she” Manfred is referring too in the second act, scene two, whom he accused himself with having killed and whom he invokes and brings onto the stage later on in the poem. She is the woman he loved, and he dragged her into his attempt to conquer the supernatural world behind the surface of things, and he considers this to be a crime that is unforgivable, and this Astarte when brought up in his presence by himself, refuses to stay, speak and say anything. She rejects him into his guilt. <br /><br />He is not Faustus because he cannot travel in time except via the illusion of some hallucination. He is not Faustus because he dragged the woman he loved into his own attempts to conquer the over-world, the other-world, the underworld, and she died of it as if it were a disease (note the allusion to the black plague, ‘Black Death’, in the 14th-15th centuries). He destroyed the woman he loved but in a very perverse way, with supernatural wizardly powers. He is not Faustus because he does not meet with forgiveness from a jury of women in heaven. He is not Faustus because he does not find any salvaging mission on earth, and what’s more, he is more fatal or lethal to people he meets than a real curse on them would be. As for that, Count as he may be, he is not Count Dracula because he has no eternity. Eternity is for him a pure discursive illusion. <br /><br />What kind of a man is he then? He is a man bored with humdrum aristocratic idle life with many servants and even slaves. He is a man who is trying to call magical if not witchlike actions to entertain his boredom. He is suicidal because courting with death is entertaining for his idle aristocratic mind if we can call his disturbed consciousness a mind or a soul. He is definitely psychotic. “Different,” he says he is, unable to integrate the real world, unable to accept the real world the way it is, he wants to live in his own different world and thus escape the real world, and for a while, he believes this would make him escape death. He is in fact on the verge of schizophrenia, more paranoid deep under than just plain deranged. How did he develop this paranoia is not said and difficult to imagine? And this makes him suicidal to the point of actually dying by mentally killing himself. This is banal today when we know what happens to some people who suddenly abandon the world completely, abandon themselves to dying, and let themselves die, make themselves die. There are of course many other suicidal endings, including by self-poisoning with alcohol or drugs. And Byron knew everything he had to know about drugs and opium. <br /><br />This poem then is extremely pessimistic on the individual animal called man. Isolated in a tower, in a castle, in a social aristocratic ghetto, in the mountains which are nothing but a witch, that individual human will end up killing himself, be it only with diabetes for over-eating (obesity) and the consumption of too much sugar of any type, including plain potato starch. In other words, this dramatic poem is a perfect pamphlet for suicide and death – or against it: humanity has no future because its individuals, particularly its elite have no future but suicide in a way or another. <br /><br />Beautiful poetry and tragic dramatic mesmerizing call for suicide.<br /><br />Dr. Jacques COULARDEAU<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14850176354849181903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3226222841" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3226222841" />
    <a title="classic reverie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37374526-classic-reverie"><img alt="classic reverie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480288607p2/37374526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3226222841?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="classic reverie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="classic reverie" href="/user/show/37374526-classic-reverie">classic reverie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=poems">poems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=english-writer">english-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=1800">1800</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=referenced-in-a-booking-i-am-readin">referenced-in-a-booking-i-am-readin</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=lord-byron">lord-byron</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3226222841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3781053654492905141">I had never read Lord Byron before but decided to read his "Manfred", after a character in Balzac's Honorine is described as a kind of Manfred. When I read that passage, I had no idea what Balzac was trying to say but after reading this poetic play, I can see that both characters regret and have angst for a wrong that they did to the woman they loved, and trying to live with it. <br/><br/>Before reading Byron I was feeling a little intimidated, at the thought of his works being too highbrow for me, that I</span>
  <span id="freeText3781053654492905141" style="display:none">I had never read Lord Byron before but decided to read his "Manfred", after a character in Balzac's Honorine is described as a kind of Manfred. When I read that passage, I had no idea what Balzac was trying to say but after reading this poetic play, I can see that both characters regret and have angst for a wrong that they did to the woman they loved, and trying to live with it. <br /><br />Before reading Byron I was feeling a little intimidated, at the thought of his works being too highbrow for me, that I might not understand his poem but I just started reading and I understood it pretty well. After reading I wanted to see if my ideas were in line with the general line, so I looked it up and I hit the mark. Some added information from the Internet about Byron's life and the poem.<br /><br />From Wikipedia below-<br /><br />" Byron wrote this "metaphysical drama", as he called it, after his marriage to Annabella Millbanke failed because of a scandal due to charges of sexual improprieties and an incestuous affair between Byron and his half-sister, Augusta Leigh. Attacked by the press and ostracised by London society, Byron fled England for Switzerland in 1816 and never returned. At the time, he was living at the Villa Diodati in Switzerland.<br /><br />Because Manfred was written immediately after this, and because it regards a main character tortured by his own sense of guilt for an unmentionable offence, some critics consider it to be autobiographical, or even confessional.[2] The unnamed but forbidden nature of Manfred's relationship to Astarte is believed to represent Byron's relationship with his half-sister Augusta.<br /><br />Most of Manfred was written on a tour through the Bernese Alps in September 1816. The third act was rewritten in February 1817 since Byron was not happy with its first version."<br /><br /><br />From Balzac's Honorine, that mentions Manfred and Lord Byron.<br /><br />"“‘Do you know why I like Lord Byron so much? It is because he suffered as animals do. Of what use are complaints when they are not an elegy like Manfred’s, nor bitter mockery like Don Juan’s, nor a reverie like Childe Harold’s? Nothing shall be known of me. My heart is a poem that I lay before God.’"<br /><br />"He was a sort of catholic Manfred, and unstained by crime, carrying his choiceness into his faith, melting the snows by the fires of a sealed volcano, holding converse with a star seen by himself alone! "<br /><br /><br />Poem in short-A man trying to forget a wrong done to one he loved and try to obtain forgiveness.<br /><br />❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌spoiler alert❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌❌<br /><br />Manfred tries to have supernatural spirits help him to forget how he hurt the woman he loved and try to get her spirit to forgive but she does not answer. <br />Count Octave from Balzac's story feels responsible for his wife's descent and loves her so deeply but she refuses to see him. Tragedy for all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3781053654492905141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6319251370422049227" style="display:none">The Byronic hero! This was a fantastic poem - it was so similar to prose that it felt like reading a novel, and as vivid as watching a play; which I expect is the effect Byron intended.<br /><br />Being in the realm of Romanticism, "Manfred" is filled with emotion and feeling, and contemplations of nature and the supernatural. <div id="review_51444702" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51444702" />
    <a title="Rosemary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/133127-rosemary"><img alt="Rosemary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345597674p2/133127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51444702?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rosemary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rosemary" href="/user/show/133127-rosemary">Rosemary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51444702" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer916124809909045783">The plot: "Manfred"s title character broods over mysterious feelings of despair and ennui, calls up spirits of Nature, persuades the forces of evil to call up his dead love in a futile attempt to set his soul at peace. The drama ends with his death, of course, and his friends wonder where his soul is headed -- "whither? I dread to think." <br/><br/>The appeal: How could such a despicably loathsome rake like Lord Byron write such exquisite poetry? The lines in Manfred are keen and clear, lyrical and lovely</span>
  <span id="freeText916124809909045783" style="display:none">The plot: "Manfred"s title character broods over mysterious feelings of despair and ennui, calls up spirits of Nature, persuades the forces of evil to call up his dead love in a futile attempt to set his soul at peace. The drama ends with his death, of course, and his friends wonder where his soul is headed -- "whither? I dread to think." <br /><br />The appeal: How could such a despicably loathsome rake like Lord Byron write such exquisite poetry? The lines in Manfred are keen and clear, lyrical and lovely. Reading this makes me feel like I've eaten something utterly delicious. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="916124809909045783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2356836526" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12046110247799237169">I am going to hear Tchaikovsky's Manfred Symphony at the Chicago Symphony in a few weeks. I thought this would be a good opportunity to read the Byron, since I would probably never think to pick it up otherwise. <br/><br/>It was a very strange, quasi-Gothic quasi-Romantic read. It suffered, through no fault of its own, from my recently having read The Tragical History of Doctor Faustus by Christopher Marlowe for a class. So much of the Byron piece seems modeled on the Marlowe. The way that Manfred denies </span>
  <span id="freeText12046110247799237169" style="display:none">I am going to hear Tchaikovsky's Manfred Symphony at the Chicago Symphony in a few weeks. I thought this would be a good opportunity to read the Byron, since I would probably never think to pick it up otherwise. <br /><br />It was a very strange, quasi-Gothic quasi-Romantic read. It suffered, through no fault of its own, from my recently having read The Tragical History of Doctor Faustus by Christopher Marlowe for a class. So much of the Byron piece seems modeled on the Marlowe. The way that Manfred denies himself redemption even though it is held out to him at the last moment, seems very Faustian...not the Goethe Faust but the Marlowe Faust.<br /><br />The sin that Manfred has committed is tantalizingly vague. I think it is a drawback in making the work more compelling. If we had a sense of what he felt he had committed, something so heinous that he denies himself redemption, then perhaps Manfred would have been a more identifiable character and not just a cheap Byron knock-off.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12046110247799237169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15903618375625222645">​<i>
  <b>"I have not been thy dupe, nor am thy prey<br/>But was my own destroyer, and will be<br/>My own hereafter."</b>
</i><br/><br/>It was the first lengthy Byron poem I've read. Manfred is very poetic and it is really easy to find lines worthy of underlining. I found the way the theme of immortality was handled very original and impressive and the ending was satisfying. But the majority of the poem is very vague with very few things explained, so reading it without any knowledge of the plot would be a bit difficult.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3580672749" />
    <a title="Eliana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44291702-eliana"><img alt="Eliana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598830623p2/44291702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3580672749?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eliana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eliana" href="/user/show/44291702-eliana">Eliana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44291702-eliana?shelf=for-school">for-school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44291702-eliana?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3580672749&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3580672749" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13721223884710638072">I do not know what I just read. I am torn. He toys with biblical imagery and rejects heaven’s hope — toys with evil spirits and yet rejects hell’s despair. He goes his own way, and somehow death is relief but... is this meant to break down the dichotomy between heaven and hell? Does he create his own afterlife? Is this a commentary on hypocrisy of both the religious and the non?<br/><br/>And yet I kinda enjoyed it??? What is this sorcery!<br/><br/>Stay tuned for post-class update, maybe. Who knows.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LXOXqD7SDPOCR5T0Hw5gbJud5RG7Bxi+yadQRz+h4GDBjqTBMRgSlgJWVBVHV0Yyc4rsWa0zEucgi7Nzpq1BtA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MJxK3G931MOhQGr0J/bzrOhjsPDCMyPnKb55vqS9Y+PcYXm1YL3KpiFRqhV/r9XyAHS5uNQHKb7AkpqKPbHCNw==')})
  }

Subjected to bodily and sexual abuse by subsequent mas<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14896348377872189832\" style=\"display:none\">Born in Bermuda to a house slave in 1788, Mary Prince suffered the first of many soul-shattering experiences in her life when she was separated from her parents and siblings at the age of twelve. Subjected to bodily and sexual abuse by subsequent masters, she was bought and sold several times before she was ultimately freed.The first black woman to break the bonds of slavery in the British colonies and publish a record of her experiences, Prince vividly recalls her life in the West Indies, her rebellion against physical and psychological degradation, and her eventual escape in 1828 in England. Her straightforward, often poetic account of immense anguish, separation from her husband, and struggle for freedom inflamed public opinion during a period when stormy debates on abolition were common in both the United States and England.This edition also includes a substantial supplement by Thomas Pringle, the original editor, as well as another brief slave account: “The Narrative of Asa-Asa, a Captured African.”Essential reading for students of African-American studies, Mary Prince’s classic account of determination and endurance aids in filling the many gaps in black women’s Modern editions use a later revised version printed in 1817 and featuring a gloss. Along with other poems in Lyrical Ballads, it was a signal shift to modern poetry and the beginning of British Romantic literature.\n\nIt relates the events experienced by a mariner who has returned from a long sea voyage. The Mariner stops a man on his way to a wedding ceremony and begins to narrate a story. Two different versions of the play were published in the Jacobean era, several years later. The powerful effect of early productions of the play is indicated by the legends that quickly accrued around them—that actual devils once appeared on the stage during a performance, &quot;to the great The novel follows Victoria\'s progress from spoilt daughter of indulgent aristocrats, through a period of abuse and captivity, to a career of deepening criminality conducted under Satan\'s watchful eye. Charlotte Dacre\'s narrative deftly displays her heroine\'s movement from the vitalized position of Ann Radcliffe\'s heroines to a fully conscious commitment to vice that goes beyond that of \'Monk\' Lewis\'s deluded Ambrosio. The novel\'s most daring aspect is its anatomy of Victoria\'s intense sexual attraction to her Moorish servant Zofloya that transgresses taboos both of class and race. A minor scandal on its first publication, and a significant influence on Byron and Shelley, Zofloya has been unduly neglected. Contradicting idealized stereotypes of women\'s writing, the novel\'s portrait of indulged desire, gratuitous cruelty, and monumental self-absorption retains considerable power to disturb.<\/span>\n           $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_93157').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_93157').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron">About Lord Byron</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1568147647p3/44407.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron">Lord Byron
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5555889665430058063">George Gordon Byron (invariably known as <b>Lord Byron</b>), later Noel, 6th Baron Byron of Rochdale FRS was a British poet and a leading figure in Romanticism. Amongst Byron's best-known works are the brief poems <i>She Walks in Beauty</i>, <i>When We Two Parted</i>, and <i>So, we'll go no more a roving</i>, in addition to the narrative poems <i>Childe Harold's Pilgrimage</i> and <i>Don Juan</i>. He is regarded as one of the greatest Bri</span>
  <span id="freeText5555889665430058063" style="display:none">George Gordon Byron (invariably known as <b>Lord Byron</b>), later Noel, 6th Baron Byron of Rochdale FRS was a British poet and a leading figure in Romanticism. Amongst Byron's best-known works are the brief poems <i>She Walks in Beauty</i>, <i>When We Two Parted</i>, and <i>So, we'll go no more a roving</i>, in addition to the narrative poems <i>Childe Harold's Pilgrimage</i> and <i>Don Juan</i>. He is regarded as one of the greatest British poets and remains widely read and influential, both in the English-speaking world and beyond.<br /><br />Byron's notabilty rests not only on his writings but also on his life, which featured upper-class living, numerous love affairs, debts, and separation. He was notably described by Lady Caroline Lamb as "mad, bad, and dangerous to know". Byron served as a regional leader of Italy's revolutionary organization, the Carbonari, in its struggle against Austria. He later travelled to fight against the Ottoman Empire in the Greek War of Independence, for which Greeks revere him as a national hero. He died from a fever contracted while in Messolonghi in Greece.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5555889665430058063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
